SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
Our borings performed for the project generally encountered two distinct geologic 
subsurface profiles. The first profile is located west of 15th Street and includes the 
proposed Waimomi Substation area. The second profile is located east of 15th Street and 
includes the existing Hickam Substation. Our borings drilled to the west of 15th Street 
generally encountered a surface fill layer overlying weathered volcanic tuff, volcanic tuff 
formation, lagoonal deposits, coralline deposits/formations, and alluvial soils extending to 
the maximum depth explored of approximately 82.5 feet below the existing ground 
surface. Our borings drilled to the east of 15th Street generally encountered a surface fill 
layer overlying weathered volcanic tuff, volcanic tuff formation, and interbedded coral 
formation and coralline detritus extending to the maximum depth explored of about 81 
feet below the existing ground surface.  
Groundwater was encountered in the borings at depths ranging from about 6.3 to 
22.4 feet below the existing ground surface at the time of the field exploration. These 
groundwater levels corresponded to about Elevations -0.9 to +3.9 feet MSL. However, 
groundwater levels likely will change due to tidal fluctuations, seasonal precipitation, 
surface water runoff, and other factors considering the proximity of the project site to Pearl 
Harbor and the Pacific Ocean. 
It should be noted that loose to medium dense lagoonal deposits and coralline 
detritus encountered at the western portion of the project site are susceptible to soil 
liquefaction when subjected to ground motions associated with the maximum considered 
earthquake. Based on the potentially liquefiable soil deposits encountered, seismically 
induced ground settlements on the order of about 4 to 6 inches may be anticipated due 
to a strong seismic event associated with the maximum considered earthquake. 
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered in our borings and the presence 
of potentially liquefiable lagoonal and coralline deposits at the Waimomi Substation site, 
we recommend using a deep foundation system consisting of augered-cast-in place 
(ACIP) piles to support the new structures and equipment pads planned at the 
Waimomi Substation. In order to achieve the design load resistances imposed on the 
ACIP pile foundations, we recommend using 24-inch nominal diameter ACIP piles 
extending to 45 and 50 feet below the bottom of the pile cap (assumed to be 3 to 5 feet 
below the existing grade). The two recommended ACIP pile foundations would be 
capable of supporting allowable compressive load capacities of 190 kips (95 tons) and 
250 kips (125 tons) per pile for the 45-foot and 50-foot long ACIP piles, respectively. 
Please note that a substantial component of the allowable compressive load capacity will 
need to be reduced by the anticipated downdrag loads (70 kips) acting on the piles caused 
by the potentially liquefiable soils identified at the Waimomi Substation site. 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page v 
Hawaii • California 
Cavities and voids are commonly encountered in the coralline deposits in the 
project vicinity. Therefore, the actual volume of grout required to fill the ACIP piles may 
be appreciably more than the theoretical grout volume. Based on our experience with 
coralline deposits in the area, we envision an average grout factor (overpour) of about 30 
to 70 percent over the theoretical grout volume may be required for the ACIP piles at the 
Waimomi Substation site. 
Based on our evaluation of the subsurface conditions and the transmission line 
pole structure foundation information provided to us, we understand drilled shafts with 
diameters of 48, 60, and 72 inches in diameter will be used to support the transmission 
line pole structures and dead-end pole structures. Based on the subsurface conditions 
encountered, we anticipate the drilled shaft installation will require an experienced drilled 
shaft subcontractor to install the drilled shaft foundations due to the presence of the soft 
and/or loose lagoonal deposits and shallow groundwater conditions. Therefore, 
consideration should be given to requiring pre-qualification of the drilled shaft 
subcontractor. 
Design of metallic substructures, such as metallic piping and embedded pole 
substructures, should consider the effects of the corrosive environment on the 
substructure. Based on the laboratory corrosivity test results, the subsurface soils at the 
project site may be classified as Corrosion Category A (moderately to severely corrosive) 
using the City and County of Honolulu, Board of Water Supply (BWS) guidelines. 
Therefore, it may be appropriate to consult with a professional corrosion engineer to 
review the test results and provide detailed recommendations for corrosion protection. 
The text of this report should be referred to for detailed discussions and specific 
geotechnical recommendations for design of the project. 
END OF SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS

SECTION 1.  GENERAL 
This report presents the results of our geotechnical engineering exploration 
performed for the proposed Dry Dock 5 Substation and Transmission Line Structures 
project at Joint Base Pearl Harbor-Hickam in Honolulu on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. The 
project location and general vicinity are shown on the Project Location Map, Plate 1. 
This report summarizes the findings from our field exploration and laboratory 
testing and presents our geotechnical recommendations derived from our analyses for 
the design of the proposed project. These recommendations are intended for the design 
of foundations, site grading, underground utilities, and corrosion protection only. The 
findings and recommendations presented herein are subject to the limitations noted at 
the end of this report. 
1.1 
Project Considerations 
The project site is located at the Joint Base Pearl Harbor-Hickam (JBPHH) 
complex in Honolulu on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. Based on the information provided, 
we understand two substations and several electrical transmission lines are planned to 
support the new Dry Dock 5 Project. We understand that approximately 21 new 
transmission line pole structures are proposed throughout JBPHH to connect existing 
electrical infrastructure to the two new substations proposed for the project. The proposed 
transmission lines generally will connect to the existing transmission lines on 
Kamehameha Highway and will follow the proposed routes along Center Drive, 
Porter Avenue, 17th Avenue, South Avenue, and Lake Erie Street. 
Generally, the proposed Waimomi Substation is bounded by Central Avenue to the 
north, Lake Erie Street to the east, an existing on-grade parking lot to the south, and 
contractor storage areas to the west, respectively. We understand the substation 
generally will consist of equipment pads for various electrical components, such as the 
transformer, control house, 46 KV breaker pad, steel structure piers, and switchgear, 
among others.

1.2 
Purpose and Scope 
The purpose of our geotechnical engineering exploration was to obtain an 
overview of the surface and subsurface conditions to develop a soil and/or rock data set 
to formulate geotechnical recommendations for design of the proposed project. In order 
to accomplish this scope, we conducted an exploration program consisting of the 
following tasks and efforts: 
1. 
Procurement of base passes for our field personnel and subcontractors for 
access to the project site. 
2. 
Prepare and submit a Safety Plan/Accident Prevention Plan (APP) with 
Activity Hazard Analysis (AHA) in support of our field exploration activities 
and briefing of our field personnel of the plan. 
3. 
Application for the NAVFAC Dig Permit and utility clearance at the proposed 
boring locations. 
4. 
Staking out of boring locations, coordination, and performance of 
underground utility line toning. 
5. 
Provision of a dedicated Site Safety and Health Officer (SSHO) during our 
field exploration work. 
6. 
Provision of traffic control plans and traffic control for borings located along 
existing roadways. 
7. 
Preparation and submittal of a Contractor Crane Entry Package and 
arrangement of a P-2 crane lift inspection with NAVFAC prior to our 
mobilization.  
8. 
Mobilization and demobilization of a truck-mounted drill rig, work support 
truck, water truck, and two operators to the project site and back. 
9. 
Drilling and sampling of 40 borings extending to depths of about 40 to 
86.5 feet below the existing ground surface for a total of about 2,522 linear 
feet of field exploration for the geotechnical investigation. 
10. 
Backfilling of the drilled borings with cement-bentonite grout upon 
completion of each boring. 
11. 
Performance of one double ring infiltrometer test to evaluate the infiltration 
characteristics of the subsurface materials at the Waimomi Substation by 
our engineer/geologist. 
12. 
Performance of in-situ soil resistivity surveys at two selected locations using 
the Wenner 4-Pin Method along two lines at 1.25, 5, 7.5, 10, 15, 20, 25, 30,

Miscellaneous work efforts such as drafting, word processing, and clerical 
support.

SECTION 2.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
Of interest to our geotechnical engineering analysis are the subsurface materials 
encountered at the project site, the engineering properties of the materials encountered, 
and the variability of the subsurface conditions across the project site. Therefore, the 
following subsections provide a description of the geologic setting of the project site, the 
surface and subsurface conditions encountered at the site, and a discussion on the items 
needed for seismic design, such as the seismicity, soil liquefaction, and soil profile 
characteristics for seismic analysis. 
2.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Oahu was built by the extrusion of basaltic lavas from two shield 
volcanoes, Waianae and Koolau. The older Waianae Volcano is estimated to be middle 
to late Pliocene in age and forms the bulk of the western one-third of the island. The 
younger Koolau Volcano is estimated to be late Pliocene to early Pleistocene (Ice Age) 
in age and forms the majority of the eastern two-thirds of the island. The Koolau Shield 
reached the end of its main shield-building phase about 2 million years ago. 
The geologic conditions underlying the project site are the result of a complex 
series of deposition and erosional events brought on by glacio-eustatic sea level changes 
and post-erosional volcanic activities during the Pleistocene Epoch. Following the 
cessation of volcanic activity of the Koolau Volcano, alluvial soil materials were deposited 
in a deltaic or estuarine environment in the Pearl Harbor area. 
The sea level fluctuated several times, receding perhaps 300 feet below and rising 
about 100 feet above the present sea level. Concurrent with a rise in sea level during the 
late Pleistocene era, eruption of the Salt Lake-Makalapa member of the Honolulu 
Volcanic Series mantled the older alluvium with thick deposits of volcanic ash, which 
consolidated to form volcanic tuff (locally known as “mudrock”) layers. Subsequent 
erosion dissected and thinned those mantles of volcanic tuff.  
During the recent epoch, as sea level rose to its current stand, the area was 
covered with soft and loose lagoonal marine deposits. Subsequently, these deposits were 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10

Page 5 
Hawaii • California 
covered with man-made fills placed during the middle portion of the last century for land 
reclamation projects during construction of the existing Pearl Harbor Naval Shipyard. The 
waterfront facility project site is located at the edge of the former Pearl Harbor shoreline. 
The subsurface conditions of the project site generally consist of fill materials overlying 
coralline deposits and highly weathered volcanic tuff materials underlain by alluvial soils 
at greater depths. 
2.2 
Existing Site Conditions 
The project site is located at the Joint Base Pearl Harbor-Hickam (JBPHH) in 
Honolulu on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. The project site generally is located along the 
Kamehameha Highway (Hawaii State Route 99) inbound lane and follows the proposed 
transmission line routes along Center Drive, Porter Avenue, 17th Avenue, South Avenue, 
and Lake Erie Street. The project location and general vicinity are shown on the Project 
Location Map, Plate 1. 
Based on our field observations, the proposed project site generally is covered by 
asphaltic concrete pavement, topsoil, and short grasses. The project site generally slopes 
down from the easternmost end of the project site toward the west with ground surface 
elevations ranging from about +47 to +8 feet Mean Sea Level (MSL). 
2.3 
Subsurface Characteristics 
In general, we observed five main stratigraphic units across the project area within 
the proposed dry dock substation and transmission line structures project alignments. 
The stratigraphic units consist of soil and rock materials of varying character and 
engineering properties. The five main stratigraphic units are listed below in general order 
of geologic age, beginning with the youngest unit and ending with the oldest unit: 
1. 
Recent Fill (Rf) 
2. 
Recent Alluvium (Ra) 
3. 
Quaternary Honolulu Tuffs (Qht) 
4. 
Coral Limestone (Qls) 
5. 
Older Alluvium (Qa) 
It should be noted that the surface fills generally are underlain by interbedded 
layers of volcanic tuffs, coralline detritus, coral/sandstone formations, and older alluvium. 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024

The following subsections briefly describe the general characteristics of each of the above 
stratigraphic units based on our literature research, experience with similar materials, and 
the field exploration and laboratory testing program conducted at selected locations 
across the project site. The approximate delineation of these stratigraphic units at the 
ground surface of the project vicinity is presented on the Geology Map, Plate 4. 
2.3.1 Recent Fill (Rf) 
This stratigraphic unit represents low-lying areas that were filled during 
urbanization. The majority of these low-lying areas typically are underlain at 
relatively shallow depths by recent alluvium and were originally marsh areas. 
Some parts of the filled lands are underlain by coralline deposits and/or older 
alluvial soil deposits. In areas underlain by the recent alluvium, the fill materials 
generally consist of elastic silts and sandy silts with stiff to hard consistencies. 
The quality and composition of the fill materials are variable. These fills were 
frequently placed without proper compaction control. Therefore, it is difficult to 
make any general statement regarding their engineering properties or construction 
in these areas. 
2.3.2 Recent Alluvium (Ra) 
These soils generally are the result of sedimentation in a very low energy 
environment such as an estuary or bay. Frequently, the soils assigned to this unit 
accumulated very rapidly under water and might not have been exposed to air. 
Therefore, there is a tendency to encounter under-consolidated [OCR (Over 
Consolidation Ratio) < 1.0] sediments, which settle under the weight of minimal 
overburden or structural loading. Recent alluvium generally is found within stream 
channels and bordering some stream banks, at the mouths (outlets) of streams, 
and within broad valley floors and basin features surrounded by hills. These soils 
also may be found in proximity to swamp and tidal flat environments. The soils 
assigned to this unit generally are considered poorly suited for use as support for 
foundation loads, due to their low in-situ strength characteristics and the tendency 
for excessive settlement to occur in structures overlying soils of this unit. The 
following subunits are used in this geotechnical engineering report.  
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024

Lagoonal Deposits 
This subunit generally consists of light gray to medium gray sandy silts or 
silty sands in a very soft to soft consistency and/or very loose to loose 
relative density. Gravel-sized fragments are rare. Frequently, the silts are 
organic in origin and may have a mild to strong organic odor. 
Marsh and Estuarine Deposits 
This subunit consists of dark gray and brown silts and clays with fibrous 
organic materials in a very soft to soft consistency. The marsh and/or 
estuarine deposits also contain traces of fine to medium grained sands. 
They are characterized by a moderate to strong organic odor and may 
contain small fragile mollusk shells. 
2.3.3 Quaternary Honolulu Tuffs (Qht) 
Tuff from vents of the Honolulu Volcanic Series consists of volcanic ash, which 
consolidated or hardened to form a medium hard rock that resembles siltstone or 
sandstone, locally called “mudrock.” The tuff is frequently found overlying coralline 
deposits or older alluvium units. This formation has relatively low permeability, and 
groundwater generally was not encountered in this formation. When it does occur, 
it is usually in the form of seepage along joints. 
2.3.4 Coral Limestone (Qls) 
This unit consists of fossil remnants of coral-algal reefs and their related detrital 
debris deposits. In Hawaii, the detrital deposits tend to be the dominant portion of 
any coralline rock assemblage. The detrital deposits vary from weakly to strongly 
cemented coralline debris consisting of gravel, sand, and silt-sized clastic 
fragments. Some of the coralline fragments were dissolved, and the resulting 
solution cavities were filled with cemented and/or uncemented fine sands and silts.  
Much of the subsurface conditions at the western portion of the project site and 
surrounding area consist of coralline deposits, as indicated on the Geology Map 
(Plate 4). Due to the depositional nature of coralline deposits, there is a wide range 
of material porosities and degrees of cementation in the coralline deposits resulting 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 8 
Hawaii • California 
in very wide ranges of engineering properties, such as their strength and material 
permeability characteristics. It is very difficult to obtain an undisturbed sample of 
the coralline materials for laboratory testing of strength. Therefore, laboratory tests 
conducted on small intact samples of the coralline deposits likely are not 
representative of the overall coralline deposit mass considering the significant 
range of porosities and degrees of cementation of the coralline deposit. The 
following subunits are used in this geotechnical engineering report. 
Coralline Detritus 
This subunit consists of granular detritus broken from reef formations. The 
soils generally consist of medium dense to dense, poorly graded sands and 
gravel. Silts and clays are present only in minor or trace amounts. Detritus 
also may be weakly cemented with occasional moderate to strong 
cementation. 
Coral Formation 
This subunit is composed of coral and calcareous algae in their original 
growth positions or detrital deposits, which have become moderately to well 
cemented. 
Sandstone 
Sandstone is a sedimentary rock composed of mainly cemented sand-sized 
particles (clasts) consisting of individual mineral particles or grains of the 
weathered parent rock. The sedimentary particles comprising sandstone 
typically are derived from the breakdown of parent material into sedimentary 
particles. The sedimentary particles typically are eroded, transported, and 
sorted by water or air, and deposited as layers of sedimentary materials. 
Over time, the sediments may be buried by other sediments or geologic 
layers, and the burial pressure combined with a cementing agent act to 
consolidate the grains and cement the pore space between the grains to 
form hardened sandstone. Typical cementing agents may be calcium 
carbonate (common in calcareous sand deposits of Hawaii) or silica and 
some clays.  
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 9 
Hawaii • California 
Most sandstones in Hawaii are light brown in color and calcareous being 
derived from the breakdown of parent coralline and reef materials into 
sand-sized particles. The cementing agent in most calcareous sandstone is 
calcium carbonate. Inclusions of basaltic rock fragments, marine shells, and 
other fragments of broken coral may be cemented in the sandstone layers. 
Sandstones containing such clast inclusions commonly are referred to as 
“beachrock” in Hawaii, having been derived from available shoreline 
geologic materials.  
The strength (hardness) of sandstone generally depends on the grain size, 
degree of sorting, and degree of cementation of the sedimentary materials. 
The weathering and decomposition of calcareous sandstone may be 
accelerated by exposure to acidic water, which attacks the calcareous 
cement binding the sand grains and causes a slow exfoliation of the 
outermost exposed layers of the sandstone outcropping. Sandstone may or 
may not contain visible bedding surfaces. Aeolian (wind derived) sandstone 
may exhibit cross-bedding structures that developed during the initial 
deposition of the sand layers. 
2.3.5 Older Alluvium (Qa) 
Older alluvial deposits are generally very stiff to hard silty and clayey soils that 
originally were deposited in alluvial environments, buried over long periods of time 
by more recent deposits to become consolidated, and then subsequently 
uncovered at the ground surface by erosion or other geologic processes. This unit 
generally overlies the Koolau Basalt and consists of terrigenous sediments, which 
have been transported by stream action from the upper reaches of the streams.  
Gravel lenses representing buried stream channels are sometimes encountered. 
Portions of the Older Alluvium unit contain some weathered basaltic cobbles and 
boulders. These coarse-grained parts of the unit also may contain artesian 
groundwater from the basal freshwater system or as a sub-aquifer. The deposits 
may resemble saprolitic soil deposits, but they are clearly differentiable by the 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 10 
Hawaii • California 
layered characteristics. Frequently, these materials show a moderate to high 
shrink/swell potential when subjected to fluctuations in moisture. 
Older Alluvium generally exhibit high strength, which leads to a relatively high 
bearing or load carrying capacity with generally low settlement characteristics. 
Frequently, these materials show a moderate to high shrink/swell potential when 
subjected to fluctuations in moisture content. However, this is not considered for 
the type of structural systems proposed for this project. Structurally, the Older 
Alluvium materials are considered to be sufficiently competent to support relatively 
large structural loads with low settlement characteristics when designed properly. 
The following subunits are used in this geotechnical engineering report. 
Older Alluvium 
This subunit generally consists of tan to brown fat clays with varying 
amounts of sands and infrequent pockets of gravel and cobbles. The 
consistency is generally in the range of very stiff to hard.  
Old Lagoonal Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of light gray to medium gray sandy silts or 
silty sands in stiff to very stiff consistency and/or medium dense to dense 
relative density with various degrees of cementation. In some cases, 
strongly cemented deposits form siltstones. Gravel-sized fragments are 
rare. Frequently, the silts are organic in origin and may have a mild to strong 
organic odor.

2.5 
Seismic Design Considerations 
Seismic design of the project will be based on the Unified Facilities Criteria 
“Structural Engineering” (UFC 3-301-01), which references the International Building 
Code, 2018 Edition (IBC 2018) and American Society of Civil Engineers (ASCE) 7-16, 
Minimum Design Loads for Buildings and Other Structures (2016 Edition). The following 
sections provide discussions on the seismicity, soil profile type for design, and the 
potential for liquefaction at the project site. 
2.5.1 Earthquakes and Seismicity 
In general, earthquakes that occur throughout the world are caused by shifts in the 
tectonic plates. In contrast, earthquake activity in Hawaii is linked primarily to 
volcanic activity. Therefore, earthquake activity in Hawaii generally occurs before 
or during volcanic eruptions. In addition, earthquakes may result from the 
underground movement of magma that comes close to the surface but does not 
erupt. The Island of Hawaii experiences thousands of earthquakes each year, but 
most of the earthquakes are so small that they can be detected only by sensitive 
instruments. However, some of the earthquakes are strong enough to be felt, and 
a few cause minor to moderate damage. 
In general, earthquakes associated with volcanic activity are most common on the 
Island of Hawaii. Earthquakes directly associated with the movement of magma 
are concentrated beneath the active Kilauea and Mauna Loa Volcanoes on the 
Island of Hawaii. Because the majority of the earthquakes in Hawaii 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 15 
Hawaii • California 
(over 90 percent) are related to volcanic activity, the risk of seismic activity and 
degree of ground shaking diminishes with increased distance from the Island of 
Hawaii.  
The Island of Hawaii has experienced numerous earthquakes greater than 
Magnitude 5 (M5+); however, earthquakes are not confined only to the Island of 
Hawaii. To a lesser degree, the Island of Maui also has experienced earthquakes 
greater than M5+. Therefore, moderate to strong earthquakes have occurred in 
the County of Maui. The effects of earthquakes occurring on the Island of Hawaii 
and Maui may be felt on the Island of Oahu. For example, small landslides 
occurred on the Island of Oahu as a result of the Maui Earthquake of 1938 (M6.8). 
Some houses on the Island of Oahu were reportedly damaged as a result of the 
Lanai Earthquake of 1871 (M7+). 
In the last 150 years of recorded history, we are not aware of earthquakes greater 
than Magnitude 6 that have occurred on the Island of Oahu. Based on available 
information, we understand that an earthquake of about Magnitude 5.6 occurred 
on June 28, 1948 in the vicinity of the Island of Oahu, possibly along the 
hypothesized and controversial Diamond Head Fault feature. 
The Diamond Head Fault feature is believed to extend northeasterly away from the 
southeastern tip of the Island of Oahu. The Diamond Head Fault feature may be 
related to the widely documented Molokai Fracture Zone located on the sea floor 
in the vicinity of the Hawaiian Islands. Despite only the moderate tremor intensity, 
the resulting damage was reportedly widespread and included broke windows, 
ruptured masonry building walls, and a broken underground water main. In 
addition, some areas on the Island of Oahu, including the Tantalus, Iwilei, and 
Tripler areas, reported more intense ground shaking, severe enough to have 
cracked reinforced concrete. 
2.5.2 Liquefaction Potential 
Based on the International Building Code (2018 Edition), the project site may be 
subjected to seismic activity and should be evaluated for potential soil liquefaction. 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 16 
Hawaii • California 
Soil liquefaction is a condition where saturated cohesionless soils near the ground 
surface undergo a substantial loss of strength due to the build-up of excess pore 
water pressures resulting from cyclic stress applications induced by earthquakes. 
In this process, when the loose saturated sand deposit is subjected to vibration 
(such as during an earthquake), the soil tends to densify and decrease in volume 
causing an increase in pore water pressure. If drainage is unable to occur rapidly 
enough to dissipate the build-up of pore water pressure, the effective stress 
(internal strength) of the soil is reduced. Under sustained vibrations, the pore water 
pressure build-up could equal the overburden pressure, essentially reducing the 
soil shear strength to zero and causing it to behave as a viscous fluid. During 
liquefaction, the soil acquires sufficient mobility to permit both horizontal and 
vertical movements, and if not confined, will result in significant deformations. 
Soils most susceptible to liquefaction are loose, uniformly graded, fine-grained 
sands and loose silts with little cohesion. The major factors affecting the 
liquefaction characteristics of a soil deposit are as follows: 
FACTORS 
LIQUEFACTION SUSCEPTIBILITY 
Grain Size Distribution 
Fine and uniform sands and silts are more 
susceptible to liquefaction than coarse or 
well-graded sands. 
Initial Relative Density 
Loose sands and silts are most susceptible to 
liquefaction.  Liquefaction potential is inversely 
proportional to relative density. 
Magnitude and Duration 
of Vibration 
Liquefaction potential is directly proportional to the 
magnitude and duration of the earthquake. 
We evaluated the liquefaction potential of the saturated granular soils at the project 
site using the computer software program LiquefyPro (Version 5.8n) by CivilTech 
Software. Our analyses were performed on the drilled borings based on a seismic 
event of Magnitude 6 with an associated peak ground acceleration of 0.349g 
(corresponding to Site Class “D”). Based on our analysis, the very loose to loose 
lagoonal deposits and coralline detritus encountered at the Waimomi Substation, 
Radford Drive area, and the western portion of the project site (between depths of 
about 10 and 56 feet except for some more resistant zones with some cementation 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 17 
Hawaii • California 
of the subsurface materials) generally are susceptible to liquefaction when 
subjected to a peak ground acceleration of 0.349g associated with a Magnitude 6 
earthquake event. In accordance with UFC 3-220-01, Change 1 dated 
November 3, 2021, a minimum factor of safety of 1.1 was used to determine 
liquefaction susceptibility as this infrastructure project is classified as a 
Risk Category III facility.  
In the event of soil liquefaction caused by a peak ground acceleration associated 
with the Maximum Considered Earthquake (MCE) of 0.349g, the estimated 
seismically induced ground settlements due to soil liquefaction range from about 
4 to 6 inches at the substation site. The seismically induced settlement are based 
on the simplified procedures outlined by Tokimatsu and Seed (1987). 
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered in our field explorations at the 
eastern portion of the project site (excluding the Radford Drive area), the geology 
in the area, and our engineering analyses, the potential for soil liquefaction at the 
eastern portion of the project site is non-existent due to the presence of volcanic 
tuff formation. Therefore, the potential for soil liquefaction is not a design 
consideration for the eastern portion of the project site.

Waimomi Substation, Radford Drive, and Western Portion of the Project Site

Design contained in ASCE 7-16 (Minimum Design Loads for Buildings and Other 
Structures). In accordance with Chapter 20 of ASCE 7-16, a site-specific site

SECTION 3.  DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
Our field exploration generally encountered two geologic subsurface profiles for 
the project. The first subsurface profile is located west of 15th Street and includes the 
proposed Waimomi Substation area. The second subsurface profile is located east of 
15th Street and includes the existing Hickam Substation. Our borings drilled to the west 
of 15th Street generally encountered a surface fill layer overlying weathered volcanic tuff, 
volcanic tuff formation, lagoonal deposits, coralline deposits/formations, and alluvial soils 
extending to the maximum depth explored of approximately 82.5 feet below the existing 
ground surface. Our borings drilled to the east of 15th Street generally encountered a 
surface fill layer overlying weathered volcanic tuff, volcanic tuff formation, and 
interbedded coral formation and coralline detritus extending to the maximum explored 
depth of about 81 feet below the existing ground surface.  
Groundwater was encountered in the borings drilled at depths ranging from 
approximately 6.3 to 22.4 feet below the existing ground surface at the time of the field 
exploration. The measured groundwater levels corresponded to about Elevation -0.9 to 
+3.9 feet MSL. However, groundwater levels likely will change in response to tidal 
fluctuations, seasonal precipitation, surface water runoff, and other factors considering 
the proximity of the project site to Pearl Harbor and the Pacific Ocean. 
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered in our borings and the presence 
of potentially liquefiable lagoonal and coralline deposits at the Waimomi Substation site, 
we recommend using a deep foundation system consisting of augered-cast-in place 
(ACIP) piles to support the new structures and equipment pads planned at the 
Waimomi Substation. In order to achieve the design load resistances imposed on the 
ACIP pile foundations, we recommend using 24-inch nominal diameter ACIP piles 
extending to 45 and 50 feet below the bottom of the pile cap (pile caps are assumed to 
be 3 to 5 feet below the existing grade) with allowable compressive load capacities of 
190 kips (95 tons) and 250 kips (125 tons) per pile for the 45-foot and 50-foot long ACIP 
piles, respectively. Please note that a substantial component of the allowable 
compressive load capacity will need to be reduced by the anticipated downdrag loads 
(70 kips) acting on the piles caused by the potentially liquefiable soils identified at the 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 22 
Hawaii • California 
Waimomi Substation site. Cavities and voids are commonly encountered in the coralline 
deposits in the project vicinity. Therefore, the actual volume of grout required to fill the 
ACIP piles may be appreciably more than the theoretical grout volume. Our experience 
with similar type materials in the vicinity of the project site suggests an average grout 
factor (overpour) of about 30 to 70 percent over the theoretical grout volume may be 
required for the ACIP piles at the Waimomi Substation site. 
Considering the lengths and structural load capacities of the ACIP piles, we 
recommend conducting one instrumented static load test for the project. The load test 
should be conducted on a sacrificial 24-inch diameter ACIP pile extending to a depth of 
about 55 feet below the ground surface. The load test pile should be structurally reinforced 
and instrumented with embedment strain gauges for load testing purposes. The load test 
results will be used to confirm or modify the estimated lengths of production ACIP piles. 
Based on our evaluation of the subsurface conditions and the transmission line 
pole structure foundation information provided to us, we understand drilled shafts with 
diameters of 48, 60, and 72 inches in diameter will be used to support the transmission 
line pole structures and dead-end pole structures. Based on the subsurface conditions 
encountered, we anticipate the drilled shaft installation will require an experienced drilled 
shaft subcontractor to install the drilled shaft foundations due to the presence of the soft 
and/or loose lagoonal deposits and shallow groundwater conditions. Therefore, 
consideration should be given to requiring pre-qualification of the drilled shaft 
subcontractor. 
Detailed discussion and specific recommendations for the design of foundations, 
site grading, underground utility lines, corrosion protection, and other geotechnical 
aspects of the project are presented in the following sections of this report. 
3.1 
Waimomi Substation Equipment Pads and Structure Foundations 
We understand new structures to support various equipment and appurtenances 
will be required for the project. Based on the subsurface conditions encountered, the 
potential for seismically induced ground settlements on the order of about 4 to 6 inches, 
and the preliminary structural drawings and foundation loads, we recommend using a 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 23 
Hawaii • California 
deep foundation support system consisting of augered cast-in-place (ACIP) piles to 
support the new substation. ACIP piles are constructed by rotating a continuous flight 
hollow-stem auger (generally 10 to 36 inches in diameter) into the ground until either the 
planned pile tip depth or termination (refusal) criterion is achieved. At termination depth, 
grout of a specified consistency is pumped into the augered hole through an orifice 
located in the drilling bit at the tip of the hollow-stem auger. The auger is slowly withdrawn 
from the hole and the pile shaft is formed as a continuous flow of grout replaces the soil 
removed by the auger. After the auger stem is removed, the reinforcing steel cage is then 
inserted into the grouted hole (through the fluid grout) within the set-time window allowed 
by the grout.  
Based on the potentially liquefiable soil deposits encountered, seismically induced 
ground settlements on the order of about 4 to 6 inches may be anticipated due to a strong 
seismic event consistent with the Maximum Considered Earthquake. Considering the 
importance of the infrastructure (Risk Category III), we believe the estimated seismically 
induced ground settlements will not be acceptable. Therefore, consideration should be 
given to supporting the substation equipment and structures on a deep foundation system 
consisting of augered cast-in place (ACIP) piles. 
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered in the borings drilled at the 
project site, we believe ACIP piles with a diameter of 24 inches may be used effectively 
to support the new equipment pads (transformer, switchgear, breaker, and control 
enclosure pads) located at the Waimomi Substation for the project. The 24-inch ACIP 
piles may be designed based on an allowable compressive load capacity of up to 190 kips 
(95 tons) or 250 kips (125 tons) per pile for the 45-foot and 50-foot long ACIP piles, 
respectively. This pile length is based on an assumed pile cap depth of about 3 to 5 feet 
below the existing ground surface. In general, ACIP piles would need to be extended 
down through the fill, lagoonal deposits, coralline deposits, and alluvial soils. The 
following table presents the results of the anticipated 24-inch diameter ACIP pile lengths.

In general, the ACIP piles should have a minimum center-to-center spacing of 
three (3) times the pile diameter to avoid reduction in vertical load capacity due to group 
action and to facilitate drilling of the ACIP piles. The allowable compressive load capacity 
of the ACIP piles is to support dead-plus-live loads (including downdrag loads, where 
appliable) and may be increased by up to one-third (⅓) for transient loads, such as wind 
or seismic forces. A factor of safety of 2.0 was used in arriving at the allowable 
compression load provided above; therefore, a static load test will be required. 
Due to the relatively high allowable compressive load capacity with respect to the 
pile diameter, high-strength grout and steel reinforcement will be required for the ACIP 
piles in accordance with Seismic Design Category (SDC) D requirements. We 
recommend using a grout with a minimum compressive strength of 5,000 psi (pounds per 
square inch) with a water-cement ratio of 0.40 or less for the ACIP piles. The allowable 
stresses in the grout and reinforcing steel of the ACIP piles should be checked by the 
structural engineer to conform with the requirements of Section 1810.3.2.6 of IBC 2018. 
The load-bearing capacity of the ACIP piles will depend largely on the relative 
density of the soils and quality of the coralline deposits within the bearing strata. Because 
local variations in the subsurface materials may occur at the project site, it is imperative 
that a Geolabs representative (or a qualified Geotechnical QC Specialist experienced with 
installing ACIP piles) be present to observe the installation of the ACIP piles. In addition, 
contract documents should include provisions (unit prices) for additional drilling and 
extension of the ACIP pile during construction to account for unforeseen subsurface 
conditions.

Augered cast-in-place (ACIP) pile is a specialized foundation support system and 
requires an experienced subcontractor for proper installation. Therefore, we recommend 
giving consideration to requiring pre-qualification of the ACIP pile subcontractor for this 
project. The subsequent subsections address the design and construction of the ACIP 
pile foundations, which include the following: 
1. 
Downdrag Loads 
2. 
Uplift Load Resistance 
3. 
Lateral Load Resistance 
4. 
ACIP Pile Construction Considerations 
5. 
ACIP Indicator Pile Program 
6. 
Static Load Tests 
3.1.1 Downdrag Loads 
As mentioned above, the loose to very loose lagoonal deposits and coralline 
deposits at the project site are susceptible to liquefaction during a seismic event 
producing a peak horizontal ground acceleration of about 0.349g. In the event of 
seismically induced liquefaction, settlement of the loose and very loose lagoonal 
deposits and coralline deposits will occur at the project site. When the loose and 
very loose deposits move downward relative to the stationary pile, the downward 
movement will result in negative skin friction forces, generally known as downdrag 
forces, acting on the pile. The magnitude of the downdrag force depends on the 
pile perimeter, thickness of the liquefiable layer, and relative density of the 
potentially liquefiable loose lagoonal deposits. 
As mentioned previously, seismically induced ground settlements on the order of 
about 4 to 6 inches may be anticipated at the western portion of the project site 
and Pole location No. 1B (Radford Drive) caused by a strong seismic event at the 
project site. The seismically induced ground settlements will induce downdrag 
loading on the ACIP pile foundations due to the downward movement of the 
liquefiable soils during and following the seismic event. We estimate an average 
negative skin friction value of 350 psf (pounds per square foot) may be used to 
estimate the magnitude of the downdrag loading acting on the ACIP piles.  
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 26 
Hawaii • California 
For the equipment pads located at the Waimomi Substation, the ACIP piles should 
be designed to resist the downdrag loading within depths of 0 to about 32 feet 
below the ground surface. Based on our foundation analyses, we estimate the 
downdrag loads imposed on the vertical ACIP piles due to the potential for 
liquefaction of the lagoonal deposits may be on the order of about 70 kips per pile 
for the 24-inch diameter ACIP piles. We believe the downdrag loads imposed on 
the pile foundations as a result of soil liquefaction may be considered a transient 
load. However, the downdrag load also needs to be taken into account in the 
service load consisting of dead-plus-live loads plus downdrag loads. Where the 
soil profile is susceptible to soil liquefaction, the contribution to compression 
loading from the liquefiable soil layer should be neglected as the soil zone will 
contribute to negative skin friction. 
3.1.2 Uplift Load Resistance 
Uplift loads imposed on the ACIP piles may be resisted by a combination of the 
dead weight of the ACIP pile and shear along the pile surface and the adjacent 
soils. The allowable skin friction values for uplift loading for the ACIP piles at 
Waimomi Substation are provided in the Idealized Soil Profile Properties Summary 
(Plate 6.1) and may be used to estimate the allowable uplift load capacity for static 
or transient loading. The project structural engineer should check the capacity of 
the ACIP pile member in tension when used to carry tensile loads. 
Based on our analysis, an allowable uplift load capacity of about 130 kips per pile 
may be used for the design of the 24-inch diameter ACIP piles. To achieve the 
allowable uplift load capacity of 130 kips, the ACIP piles will need to be reinforced 
to a depth of 40 feet below the top of the ACIP pile to provide for proper load 
transfer to the surrounding soils. The allowable uplift load capacity provided above 
is for supporting sustained uplift loads. The project structural engineer should 
check the structural capacity of the pile member in tension, where the uplift load 
capacity of the pile is used.

3.1.3 Lateral Load Resistance 
Lateral loads imposed on the equipment pads may be resisted by a combination 
of the passive pressure acting against the near-vertical faces of the foundation 
caps and/or grade beams and the lateral load capacity of the ACIP piles. Passive 
earth pressure against the near-vertical faces of the pile caps and/or grade beams 
may be estimated using an equivalent fluid pressure of 300 pounds per square 
foot per foot of depth (pcf) and 150 pcf for above and below groundwater 
conditions, respectively. Groundwater may be assumed to be at +3 feet MSL for 
design purposes.  
In general, lateral load resistance of the ACIP piles is a function of the stiffness of 
the surrounding soil, the stiffness of the ACIP pile, allowable deflection at the top 
of pile, and induced moment in the pile. Geotechnical parameters for the soil and 
other geo-materials encountered in the subsurface for lateral load analysis at the 
Waimomi Substation are presented in the Idealized Soil Profile Properties 
Summary (Plate 6.1). 
The effect of group action was considered in our lateral load analysis for ACIP pile 
foundations with more than one pile per pile cap by including an efficiency factor 
in the direction of loading. In general, ACIP piles should have a minimum center-
to-center spacing of 8.0 times the pile diameter to avoid reduction in the lateral 
load capacity of the piles due to group action. For pile groups with ACIP pile center-
to-center spacings closer than 8.0 times the pile diameter, group reduction factors 
shall be implemented into the design of the equipment pad foundations and the 
moment demand of the piles should be increased to account for maximum induced 
moment of the pile group. The following table presents our recommendation for 
the group reduction factor and maximum induced moment demand factor.

3.1.4 ACIP Pile Construction Considerations 
The proper installation of the ACIP piles is dependent upon the procedures and 
equipment used by the foundation subcontractor. Therefore, the foundation 
subcontractor’s personnel on the project site who will be responsible for the 
installation should have the relevant experience with the augering and pumping 
equipment, experience with placing ACIP piles under similar subsurface 
conditions, as well as experience with handling the special grout mixes, 
admixtures, etc. 
The importance of grouting pressure varies with soil type and other factors. It is 
important that sufficient grout pressure is maintained to assure a constant flow of 
grout to the tip of the auger. This will allow adequate grout quantities to be built up 
on and around the auger flight. The grout build-up or “head” of grout is necessary 
to ensure an adequate grout supply to replace the volume of soil removed when 
withdrawing the auger during installation and to maintain adequate pressure to 
prevent collapsing of the sidewalls. The incremental volume of grout placed 
generally is the single most important installation control used during installation 
of the ACIP piles. Therefore, the foundation subcontractor and the on-site ACIP 
pile installation inspector need to monitor the grout volume being placed 
continuously. 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 29 
Hawaii • California 
In order to assure that the ACIP piles are installed in accordance with the project 
plans and specifications, automated monitoring equipment should be required to 
monitor the installation of the ACIP piles during construction on a real-time basis 
to provide the necessary data for evaluation and assessment. In general, the 
automated monitoring equipment selected should be capable of automatically 
monitoring and digitally recording key elements, such as the time, depth and torque 
of the auger during drilling; the time, depth, grout volume and grout pressure during 
grouting; as well as producing a real-time graph, hard copy printout, and digital 
record for evaluation and documentation. 
As indicated above, cavities and voids are commonly encountered in the coralline 
deposits in the project vicinity. Therefore, the actual volume of grout required to fill 
the ACIP piles may be appreciably more than the theoretical grout volume. Based 
on our experience with coralline deposits in the area, we envision an average grout 
factor (overpour) of about 30 to 70 percent over the theoretical grout volume may be 
required for the ACIP piles at the Waimomi Substation site. Placement of piles within 
six pile diameters should be delayed until the grout in the pile has been placed for 
12 hours or more. 
In addition to the automatic monitoring equipment discussed above, a Geolabs 
representative (or a qualified ACIP Pile “Special Inspector”) should observe the 
installation of the ACIP piles and performance of the instrumented load tests on 
the ACIP piles. It should be noted that the ACIP pile design was developed from 
our analysis using the field exploration data. Therefore, observation of the ACIP 
pile installation operations by Geolabs is a vital part of the foundation design to 
confirm the design assumptions. 
3.1.5 ACIP Indicator Pile Program 
An ACIP indicator pile program is recommended for this project considering that 
the ACIP pile foundation is a very specialized foundation system and requires an 
elevated level of expertise for proper installation. Therefore, we recommend 
undertaking an ACIP indicator pile program to fulfill the following objectives: 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 30 
Hawaii • California 
To examine the adequacy of the methods and equipment proposed 
by the contractor to install the ACIP piles into the coral ledges and 
coralline detritus deposits. 
To confirm or modify the lengths of the ACIP piles. 
To assess the contractor’s method of auger drilling and withdrawal. 
To assess the contractor’s method of grout injection. 
To achieve these objectives, we recommend the ACIP indicator pile program 
consist of drilling about 10 indicator piles (full design depth at production pile 
locations) and one dedicated load test pile (sacrificial pile at non-production pile 
location). The indicator piles may be incorporated into the actual foundation system 
upon the evaluation of the load test results. In addition, a Geolabs representative 
should be present during the indicator pile program to evaluate the contractor’s 
method of ACIP pile installation and to evaluate the subsurface materials 
encountered during the drilling operations. 
3.1.6 Instrumented Load Tests 
As part of the pre-construction activities, we recommend conducting a bi-
directional axial load test using an expandable load cell on the 24-inch diameter 
ACIP load test piles. The bi-directional load test separately tests the shear 
resistance and end-bearing components of the ACIP pile by loading the ACIP pile 
in two directions (upward for shear resistance, and downward for end-bearing and 
shear resistance). The expandable load cell should have a minimum diameter of 
12 inches and should be capable of applying a load of 350 kips in each direction 
for the 55-foot long ACIP pile. The expandable load cell will need to be attached 
to the reinforcing steel cage prior to lowering the cage into the drilled hole. A typical 
load test detail showing the location of the load cell and the recommended strain 
gauges is presented on Plate 7. 
The ACIP pile load tests should be performed in general accordance with the Quick 
Load Test Method of ASTM D1143 with the following modifications. The load test 
pile should be loaded to at least 250 percent of the allowable compressive load 
capacity of the ACIP pile to evaluate the side shear resistance and end-bearing 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 31 
Hawaii • California 
components of the ACIP pile. Installation of the expandable load cell, installation 
of the embedment strain gauges, performance of the bi-directional axial load test, 
and presentation of the load test data should be performed by a professional 
experienced in these types of load testing procedures. The 55-foot-long load test 
pile should be loaded at increments of about 12.5 kips and should be held for a 
minimum of 4 hours (at each load) at 200-kip, 250-kip, and 300-kip load increments 
to evaluate the potential for creep effects. 
A Geolabs representative should observe the installation and performance of the 
instrumented load tests on the ACIP piles. It should be noted that the ACIP pile 
design was developed from our analysis using the field exploration data. 
Therefore, observation of the ACIP pile installation operations by Geolabs is a vital 
part of the foundation design to confirm the design assumptions and should be 
designated a “Special Inspection” item according to Section 1705 of IBC 2018. 
3.2 
Drilled Shaft Foundations 
Based on the information provided, we understand the electrical transmission line 
pole structures and dead-end pole structures will be supported on cast-in-place concrete 
drilled shafts. In addition, the Radford Drive and western portion of the project site are 
susceptible to soil liquefaction and may experience seismically induced ground 
settlements on the order of about 4 to 6 inches. Therefore, new electrical transmission 
line and dead-end pole structures should be supported on a deep foundation system 
consisting of cast-in-place concrete drilled shafts. 
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered, drilled shafts extending through 
the upper fill soils and the potentially liquefiable lagoonal deposits/coralline detritus and 
embedded in the underlying coral formation, sandstone, and alluvial soil deposits (for 
Radford Drive and the western portion of the project site) and volcanic tuff (eastern portion 
of the project site) will be required to derive their axial and lateral load capacities. The 
liquefiable lagoonal deposits and loose coralline detritus have reduced strength (post-
liquefaction residual shear strengths). We understand that a detailed foundation support 
analysis will be conducted by the structural design team.

In order to develop the required bearing and lateral load resistances, the proposed 
structures may be supported by a foundation system consisting of cast-in-place concrete 
drilled shafts. The cast-in-place concrete drilled shafts would derive vertical support 
principally from skin friction between the shafts and the surrounding soils, volcanic tuff 
and/or coral formation. A factor of safety of at least 2.0 has been used to determine the 
allowable skin friction values for estimating the allowable compressive load capacity of 
the cast-in-place concrete drilled shafts. Idealized soil profiles with the recommended soil 
parameters (static and seismic conditions) for use in the design of the cast-in-place 
concrete drilled shafts at individual transmission line pole and dead-end pole structure 
locations are presented in the Idealized Soil Profile Properties Summary, Plates 6.2 
through 6.11. 
Based on our evaluation of the subsurface conditions and the transmission line 
pole structure foundation information provided to us, we understand drilled shafts with 
diameters of 48, 60, and 72 inches in diameter will be used to support the transmission 
line pole structures and dead-end pole structures. Based on the subsurface conditions 
encountered, we anticipate the drilled shaft installation will require an experienced drilled 
shaft subcontractor to install the drilled shaft foundations due to the presence of the soft 
and/or loose lagoonal deposits and shallow groundwater conditions. Therefore, 
consideration should be given to requiring pre-qualification of the drilled shaft 
subcontractor. The succeeding subsections address the design and construction of the 
drilled shaft foundations:

event at the project site. The seismically induced ground settlements will 
induce downdrag loading on the drilled shaft foundations due to the

In general, the drilled shafts should have a minimum center-to-center

3.2.4 Drilled Shaft Construction 
The performance of the drilled shafts will depend significantly upon the 
contractor’s method of construction and construction procedures. As a 
result, a Geolabs representative or a qualified Geotechnical Quality Control 
(QC) Specialist should be present to observe the installation of the drilled 
shafts during construction. In our opinion, the following may have a 
significant impact on the effectiveness and cost of the drilled shaft 
foundation. 
Where foundations are planned next to existing underground (UG) facilities, 
One Call Center notification and utility toning should be conducted to 
identify accurately the location of the underground utility lines. Potholing and 
vacuum excavation should be considered to determine the exact locations 
of the underground facilities, as as-built plans do not necessarily reflect the 
final exact location of the underground facility. 
Granular materials typically are used as bedding and the initial trench 
backfill for underground facilities; therefore, a temporary steel casing likely 
will be necessary to maintain the integrity of the sidewalls of the drilled hole 
to prevent cave-ins from the granular materials used as bedding and initial 
trench backfill. Typically, the temporary steel casing is about 6 inches larger 
in diameter than the specified drilled shaft diameter to ensure that the 
minimum drilled shaft diameter is achieved. A minimum setback distance of 
one diameter spacing between the closest edge of the underground facility 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 36 
Hawaii • California 
and the edge of the temporary steel casing should be adequate to prevent 
additional stresses imposed onto the underground facilities due to 
construction. 
The load carrying capacity of the drilled shaft depends, to a large extent, on 
the friction developed between the shaft and the surrounding soils or 
geologic formation. Therefore, proper construction techniques are 
important. The contractor should exercise care in drilling or excavating the 
shaft hole and in placing concrete in the hole. 
Based on our field exploration, difficult drilling conditions likely will be 
encountered at the project site due to the varying geologic materials 
encountered and should be expected. The drilled shaft subcontractor will 
need to have the appropriate equipment and tools to drill through hard coral 
formations, sandstones, and volcanic tuff formations encountered in the 
borings. 
Based on our field exploration, the project site is underlain by soft and/or 
loose lagoonal deposits and coralline detritus extending to depths up to 
approximately 32 feet below the existing ground surface. Due to the soft 
consistency and/or loose relative density of these materials, caving-in 
and/or sloughing of these materials likely will occur during the drilling 
operations. To reduce the potential for caving-in of the drilled holes, 
temporary casing should be provided during the drilled shaft installation 
and/or the use of drilling fluids (polymer slurry) may be necessary to 
maintain the integrity of the drilled hole during drilled shaft installation. 
Typically, the temporary steel casing is about 6 inches larger in diameter 
than the specified drilled shaft diameter to ensure that the specified 
minimum drilled shaft diameter is achieved. Larger diameter drilled shafts 
with sizes of 60, 72, or 84 inches may be required for the transmission line 
pole structures and dead-end pole structures.  
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 37 
Hawaii • California 
Care should be exercised during removal of the temporary casing to reduce 
the potential for “necking” of the drilled shaft. Therefore, a minimum 5-foot 
head of fluid concrete above the bottom of the casing or adequate concrete 
head to counter the hydrostatic pressures due to the shallow groundwater 
conditions should be maintained during removal of the casing. The shallow 
groundwater conditions at the project site may pose construction difficulties 
because proper observation of the sides and bottoms of the drilled shaft 
may not be possible. 
Drilling by methods utilizing drilling fluids may have a significant effect on 
the supporting capacity of the drilled shaft; therefore, use of drilling fluids 
would require prior evaluation and acceptance by Geolabs. If drilling fluids 
are proposed by the drilled shaft subcontractor, a dedicated load test shaft 
for load testing purposes to evaluate the effect of the drilling fluid on the 
capacity of the drilled shaft will be required. 
Because of the shallow groundwater conditions anticipated within the 
depths of the drilled shaft excavations, concrete placement by tremie 
methods will be required during drilled shaft construction. The concrete 
should be placed in a suitable manner by displacing the water in an upward 
fashion from the bottom of the drilled hole. A low-shrink concrete mix with 
high slump (7 to 9-inch slump range) should be used to provide close 
contact between the drilled shafts and the surrounding soils and geologic 
formations. Concrete should be placed in a suitable manner to reduce the 
potential for segregation of the aggregates from the concrete mix. The 
concrete mix should have a minimum compressive strength of 5,000 psi 
(pounds per square inch) with a low water-cement ratio of 0.40 or lower 
considering the brackish groundwater conditions at the site.  
In addition, concrete should be placed promptly after drilling (within 
24 hours after substantial completion of the drilled holes) to reduce the 
potential for softening/unraveling of the sides of the drilled holes. 
Furthermore, drilling adjacent to a recently constructed shaft (within six

shaft diameters of the recently constructed drilled shaft) should not 
commence until the concrete for the recently constructed drilled shaft has 
cured for a minimum of 24 hours. 
A Geolabs representative or a qualified QC specialist should be present at 
the project site to observe the drilling and installation of the drilled shafts 
during construction. Although the drilled shafts are primarily designed 
based on skin friction, the bottom of the drilled hole should be relatively free 
of loose materials prior to placement of concrete. Therefore, observation of 
the drilled shaft installation operations by a Geolabs representative (or a 
qualified Geotechnical QC Specialist) is necessary to confirm the assumed 
subsurface conditions and should be designated a “Special Inspection” item 
in accordance with Section 1705 of the International Building Code (2018 
Edition). 
3.3 
Site Grading 
Based on the current design concept and existing topography, we anticipate only 
minor site grading and earthwork consisting of cuts and fills of about 2 feet or less would 
be required for construction of the project. In addition, we anticipate utility trench 
excavation and backfilling of up to about 5 to 8 feet below grade may be required. Items 
of earthwork that are addressed in the following subsections include the following: 
1. 
Site Preparation 
2. 
Fills and Backfills 
3. 
Fill Placement and Compaction Requirements. 
4. 
Excavations 
A Geolabs representative or a qualified Geotechnical QC specialist experienced 
with the local subsurface materials should observe the earthwork construction for the 
project. It is important that our representative or a qualified Geotechnical QC Specialist 
observe the site preparation to evaluate whether undesirable materials are encountered 
during the excavation and filling process and whether the exposed subsurface conditions 
are similar to those encountered and assumed in the design.

W.O. 8462-00 & 10

3.3.1 Site Preparation 
At the on-set of earthwork, areas within the contract grading limits should be 
cleared thoroughly. Vegetation, existing pavements, debris, and other unsuitable 
materials should be removed and disposed of properly off-site to reduce the 
potential for contamination of the excavated materials designated to be re-used as 
fills and/or backfills. If soft and/or wet soils are encountered during clearing, 
over-excavation may be required to remove the soft and/or wet materials to expose 
firm and/or dense soils. The resulting over-excavation should be backfilled with 
compacted fill materials. 
Existing utilities to be abandoned should be removed, and the resulting excavation 
should be properly backfilled with moisture-conditioned granular fill materials 
placed in 8-inch loose lifts and compacted to a minimum of 90 percent relative 
compaction. 
After demolition and site clearing, the exposed subgrade should be scarified to a 
minimum depth of 8 inches, moisture-conditioned to above the optimum moisture 
content, and compacted to a minimum of 90 percent relative compaction. Relative 
compaction refers to the in-place dry density of soil expressed as a percentage of 
the maximum dry density of the same soil determined in accordance with 
ASTM D1557. Optimum moisture is the water content (percentage by dry weight) 
corresponding to the maximum dry density. 
3.3.2 Fills and Backfills 
In general, the excavated on-site granular soils may be re-used as a source of fill 
materials above the groundwater level, provided that deleterious materials such as 
vegetation and/or organic matter are removed and over-sized materials greater 
than 3 inches in maximum dimension are screened. 
Excavated on-site or imported materials to be used as fill and backfill should 
consist of crushed basaltic and/or coralline aggregates. The material should be 
well-graded from coarse to fine with particles no larger than 3 inches in the largest 
dimension. In addition, the fill material should contain between 10 and 30 percent 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 40 
Hawaii • California 
fines (particles passing the No. 200 sieve). The material should have a California 
Bearing Ratio (CBR) value of 20 or more and a swell value of 1 percent or less 
when tested in accordance with ASTM D1883. 
In general, relatively clean, free-draining granular fill material (1-inch minus size 
open-graded granular materials or ASTM C33, No. 67 gradation) wrapped in a 
non-woven geotextile fabric (Mirafi 180N or equivalent) should be used for backfill 
placed below the groundwater unless efforts are made to dewater the excavations 
to enable compaction of standard backfill materials. 
Where imported fill material will be required, the imported fill material should 
conform to the requirements presented above for fill and backfill and should be 
tested to confirm that it is suitable for the intended purpose prior to delivery to the 
site. 
3.3.3 Fill Placement and Compaction Requirements 
As indicated above, fills and/or backfills below the groundwater level should consist 
of free-draining granular materials, such as ASTM C33, No. 67 gradation, as 
recommended above. Mechanical compaction of these materials will not be 
required during placement. However, densification by pounding the material with 
the bucket of a backhoe, track-walking or by other acceptable means should be 
done in a consistent manner during placement. The last lift of the material above 
the water level should be proof-rolled a minimum of eight passes with the use of a 
vibratory plate or similar vibratory equipment prior to the placement of the 
non-woven filter fabric, such as Mirafi 180N or equivalent, and capping with 
compacted fill to the finished subgrade. 
Granular fills and backfills above the water level (a minimum of 12 inches above 
the water level) should be placed in relatively level lifts not exceeding 8 inches in 
loose thickness, moisture-conditioned to above the optimum moisture content, and 
compacted to a minimum of 95 percent relative compaction. Relative compaction 
refers to the in-place dry density of soil expressed as a percentage of the maximum 
dry density of the same soil determined in accordance with ASTM D1557. 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 41 
Hawaii • California 
Optimum moisture is the water content (percentage by dry weight) corresponding 
to the maximum dry density. 
3.3.4 Excavations 
Based on our field exploration, the eastern portion of the project site generally is 
underlain by volcanic tuff formation at relatively shallow depths. Therefore, the 
contractor likely will encounter some difficult excavation conditions during 
construction where hard volcanic tuff formation is encountered. 
We anticipate that the surface fill, alluvial deposits, and coralline detritus 
encountered during our field exploration may be excavated readily with normal 
heavy excavation equipment, such as backhoe excavators. However, excavations 
extending into the volcanic tuff formation may require the use of hoerams or 
chipping. 
The above discussions regarding the rippability of the subsurface materials are 
based on our field and laboratory data from the borings drilled. Contractors should 
be encouraged to examine the site conditions and the subsurface data to make 
their own reasonable and prudent interpretation. 
3.4 
Underground Utility Lines 
We understand new underground utilities and utility line connections will be 
required for the project. We anticipate the underground utilities will be installed using 
conventional open trench (cut-and-cover) methods, and most of the trenches will be 
excavated in the existing surface fills. 
In general, granular bedding consisting of 6 inches of open-graded gravel 
(ASTM C33, No. 67 gradation) should be provided below the pipes for uniform bearing 
support. If loose and/or soft soils are encountered at the proposed inverts, an additional 
18 to 24 inches of open-graded gravel (ASTM C33, No. 67 gradation) wrapped in a 
non-woven geotextile fabric (Mirafi 180N or equivalent) may be necessary below the 
bedding material to form a bridging layer. Free-draining granular materials, such as open-
graded gravel (ASTM C33, No. 67 gradation), also should be used for the initial trench 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 42 
Hawaii • California 
backfill (pipe cover) up to about 12 inches above the crown of the pipes or above the 
groundwater level to provide adequate support around the pipes.  
Another reason for the free-draining granular materials is to reduce the need for 
substantial effort in compacting the backfill, thus reducing the potential for damage to the 
pipes. It is critical to use this free-draining material to reduce the potential for formation of 
voids below the haunches of pipes and to provide adequate support for the sides of the 
pipes. Improper backfill material around the pipes and improper placement of the backfill 
could result in backfill settlement and pipe damage. 
The upper portion of the trench backfill from the level 12 inches above the pipes 
(or groundwater) to the top of the subgrade or finished grade may consist of the excavated 
on-site materials with a maximum particle size of 3 inches or less. The backfill should be 
moisture-conditioned to above the optimum moisture, placed in about 8-inch level loose 
lifts, and mechanically compacted to at least 90 percent relative compaction. Where 
trenches are located in paved areas, the upper 3 feet of the trench backfill below the 
pavement grade should be compacted to no less than 95 percent relative compaction. 
3.5 
Field Infiltration Testing 
One double-ring infiltrometer test (designated as I-1 on Site Plan-10) was 
performed at the proposed Waimomi Substation location to determine the infiltration 
characteristics of the underlying subsurface materials. The test location (I-1) is shown on 
Site Plan-10, Plate 3.10. The test was performed in general accordance with the 
procedures outlined in ASTM D3385. Generally, the test was performed by driving two 
open concentric cylinders into the ground, partially filling the rings with water and 
maintaining a near-constant water level. 
The double-ring infiltrometer test estimates the vertical movement of water through 
the bottom of the inner cylinder, while the outer ring helps to reduce the lateral movement 
of water in the soil. The volume of water added to the inner ring is measured as the 
infiltrated water volume. The test was carried out in several increments until achieving a 
steady-state condition with a relatively constant water infiltration rate. The test was 
performed in an area that was cleared of vegetation, debris, and pavement, approximately 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024

summarized in the following table.

through the soil exposed at one location within the proposed substation, which may not 
represent the actual infiltration condition within a typical infiltration chamber footprint or 
an open basin. Due to the variability of the subsurface conditions, the absorption capacity 
of the selected storm water disposal system should be confirmed by conducting additional 
infiltration tests during construction. 
3.6 
Corrosion Potential 
Design of metallic substructures, such as electrical conduits, metallic piping, 
metallic poles embedded in the ground, etc., should consider the effects of the corrosive 
environment on the substructure. Laboratory corrosivity tests, including pH (ASTM G51), 
Minimum Resistivity (ASTM G57), Chloride Content (EPA 300.0), Sulfate Content 
(EPA 300.0), and Sulfide Content (ASTM D4658M), were performed by our office, 
Eurofins TestAmerica Laboratories, Inc., or CERCO Analytical on selected near-surface 
soil samples obtained from our field exploration for corrosivity evaluation. Results of the 
corrosivity tests are presented on Plates B-80 thru B-82 of Appendix B. 
Resistivity is recognized as one of the most significant soil characteristics 
regarding the corrosivity of the soil to buried metallic objects. Generally, the lower the 
resistivity, the greater the potential for corrosion of the buried metallic structure. 
Conversely, the higher the resistivity, the less likely the soil will contribute to the corrosion 
of metallic objects. Additionally, resistivity values of the site are used to determine the 
grounding requirements. 
A total of 51 sets of laboratory corrosivity tests were conducted for the project. The 
test results typically indicate that the subsurface materials at the project site were 
Geotechnical Engineering Report - 01/22/2024
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8462-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 44 
Hawaii • California 
corrosive to buried metallic substructures with minimum resistivity values between 
660 and 5,400 ohm-cm, except for two samples with minimum resistivity values of 10,000 
and 13,000 ohm-cm. The test results indicate that the subsurface soils are extremely 
corrosive to very corrosive (Corrosion Ratings of 1 and 2, respectively) to buried metallic 
structures based on the Board of Water Supply (BWS), City and County of Honolulu Water 
System External Corrosion Control Standards, Volume 3, 1991 Edition. However, the 
Board of Water Supply published an updated version of the standards dated July 28, 
2021, which is the version currently in use. 
The updated standards now have only two soil corrosivity categories. Corrosion 
Category A (Moderately to Severely Corrosive) applies to soil meeting any one of the 
following conditions. Corrosion Category B (Negligibly to Mildly Corrosive) is applicable 
when none of the following conditions are met. 
Resistivity ≤ 5,000 ohm-cm 
pH ≤ 6.5 
Water-soluble chloride concentration ≥ 500 ppm 
Water-soluble sulfate concentration ≥ 1,000 ppm 
Within fluctuating saltwater table 
Based on the laboratory corrosivity test results and the field resistivity test results 
(R-1 and R-2), the in-situ soils may be classified as Corrosion Category A (moderately to 
severely corrosive). Therefore, it may be appropriate to consult with a professional 
corrosion engineer to review the test results and provide detailed recommendations for 
corrosion protection. 
In addition, chloride content and sulfate content tests were performed by Eurofins 
TestAmerica Laboratories, Inc. to evaluate the corrosivity of the on-site soils encountered. 
In general, soils with a chloride content of less than 500 parts per million (ppm or mg/kg), 
sulfate content of less than 1,000 ppm, and a pH of greater than 6.5 may be considered 
"non corrosive" to underground concrete pipelines and structures. Based on the values 
of the chloride and sulfate content tests conducted on the in-situ soils, we believe that 
either Type I or Type II (Type I/II) cement may be used for the concrete in contact with 
the ground. It may be appropriate to consult with a professional corrosion engineer to 
review the test results and provide detailed recommendations for corrosion protection.
